RATING: 9
Scratches lots of itches; variable phase selection, random setup/card dealing. I agree with the late Tim Seitz; absolutely fantastic at 2p (but it's more bitter and spiteful than at larger player counts, and it's less likely to break in the same manner over and over like it seems to at higher counts), and I adore it at 3p as well. Something about Chudyk's designs seem to fall apart at higher counts. Either way, I'm honestly pressed to think of ever passing up a game if it's suggested in the 30+ times it's hit the table in 2015 alone, regardless of player count. This was the game that taught me proper "end game management" about when to end a game and how to decide that (and it's something that I find to be wonderful to tinker with, much like era C is in Indonesia); fear the merchant bonus... I do think GtR is a little more intuitive than Mottainai, and it's easier to pick up, even if there are some rough edges that Chudyk refines in his later games. Still a classic.

RATING: 10
[COLOR=#FFFFFF][BGCOLOR=#FF9933][b]aaj's FAVORITE TABLEAU BUILDER[/b][/BGCOLOR][/COLOR]
My copy is an entirely custom-printed Black Box edition. Every card icon was redrawn, along with the entire card layout, and then reprinted on Printer Studio's 310 GSM linen finish. I've also had a custom box made and made redesigned player boards at the Game Crafter. 

I like to call Glory to Rome a delicately-balanced masterpiece. Everything is so tight and well-put-together that this game really couldn't be played any other way. 

First off, let's talk about the cards. It's incredible that every card has multiple functions, that there are six types of cards in the game, each with several unique types of buildings. It's incredible that a card can be a material, a client, or a building. But more than that, it's incredible that the game is balanced through all this chaos. 

See, Glory to Rome is a tableau builder. As you complete buildings, you gain new special abilities that make your actions better, or let you break the rules a bit, or let you interferer with other players. That's crazy, and in any other game it'd be horribly broken! But in Glory to Rome, where everyone has the potential to build a crazy building (maybe even build a crazy building that nerfs your own crazy building!) the balance is maintained. Everyone is racing towards the same goal, and the end result is an immensely satisfying tableau builder where you all gradually branch out, pursuing the same goal in a million different ways. 

But secondly, this game firmly solidifies the lead/follow mechanic as one of my favorite in all of gaming ([thing=68425][/thing] is another notable example). Downtime between turns is no problem, even with five players, because you're following every one of their actions, accomplishing things even when it's not your turn. 

Sometimes, you find yourself silently (or audibly) begging with someone to lead that Craftsman because if they lead a craftsman you'll be able to set yourself up on your own turn to build a chain of two buildings. Or maybe they need to Patron so you can hire that client you desperately need...and then they don't, and you wail and curse. Or they do, and you jump up and cheer. "Player interaction"? Glory to Rome has that in spades, and more. 

And finally, this is a game that's willing to subvert its own rules. Every role can be specialized, and can be a viable path to victory. New players will often call out the Merchant role as overpowered, but there are plenty of tools in the other roles as well. Not only that, Glory to Rome provides two alternate ways to win: one instantly, without counting points (Forum/Forum Romanum), and another shiver-inducing instant end, that still forces you to count points and crown a victor (Catacombs). There are so many ways to play within, and even subvert, the delicate structure of this game. 

Glory to Rome has  metagame in spades. With experienced players, there's no game I'd rather play, and there's no game I'd rank above it in my collection. Who knew that a couple hundred cards could do so much? 

This is my forever game, till death do us part, so help me God. It's a shame that this game isn't available at retail, because my #1 game is unmatched, even by its 'spiritual' successor, Mottainai. And I'm rubbing salt in your wound by trumpeting a game that can't even be purchased. Still. Find this game, play this game, and learn to love this game. It's worth it!  

(Imperium version for life!)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Big caveat - have only played two 4 player games so far, and I really wanted to love this game. The theme is great, I love the artwork and the mechanics are sound. But I've been left underwhelmed both times, partly because of just how long it's taken to play. The first game ended on sites, the second on the orders card deck. It may well be that with more experience we can close the game down more quickly but both games over ran their welcome somewhat. We are a pretty slow group and that must have something to do with it.

The outrageously strong card combos are something I'm not sure about - in the second game I came from a slow start to be thinking with a hand size of +4, getting the effects of marble buildings before completing them, and being able to use merchants as any role and having my clients perform actions twice. It took me a fair while to get to this stage but once I did it was pretty much lights out, and I guess the other players should have submitted to my rule rather than have the game run on to the bitter end.

So far I have to say that I prefer San Juan as it seems to be to be better balanced and more disciplined as a game. Clearly GtR has a very strong following so we need to give it some further table time. I would add that my impression wasn't helped by our finishing up with a game of the Bottle Imp, which for us offered more fun and sharp decision taking in a fraction of the time. I will however be interested to read more of other people's views on GtR and may hopefully revise my rating upwards with more plays.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Glory to Rome is a fascinating beast.  It's a little hard to wrap your head around the systemic flow at first, but during your first game it should dawn on you how each role interacts with the others (though some role pairings, like Craftsman and Merchant, don't interact at all).

Glory to Rome is, first and foremost, a game of synergies.  It's about constructing buildings that give you special powers and leveraging those special powers together with other buildings to do wonderful things.  Some buildings you'll look at and say "That's lame, why would I want to build that" only to see it pair with some other cards and be blown away by how it actually plays out.

Due to the impact of these synergies, each game plays out differently and has a different feel to it.  You think different thoughts and approach the game in different ways based on the cards you have built.

You're also very concerned about what your opponents have built and how you might possibly work to counter it.

It's a fun and thoroughly engaging game.

RATING: 8
Glory to Rome is a great role selection/tableau building game with familiar elements.  It reminds me a lot of San Juan, and a little of Eminent Domain.  Similar to San Juan, you construct buildings to get their bonuses.  However, in Glory To Rome, you can't just pay for the buildings with any cards.  Instead, you must collect and use specific materials.  Also, similar to Eminent Domain, players can "follow the leader", and "boost" their roles.  It will probably replace both in my collection.

RATING: 6
An interesting card game with some fun combo-making.  Reminds me a quite a bit of Race for the Galaxy, and I prefer Race between the two.  This one feels like randomness plays more of a factor.  The game does play fast, though, which increases my tolerance for the randomness.

Additionally, Glory to Rome has more direct conflict than I generally prefer, though interestingly my group seems to rarely use the direct attack (Legionary) role, and the game doesn't seem to play any worse for it.

Update 6/11/15: Having played a few more times, my interest in the game has declined.  In my last game, I built a total of two buildings and yet won handily on the strength of cards in my vault.  Throughout the game I felt like I was doing very little and was way behind, which ended up not being the case.  It was a very unsatisfying win.  It could be argued my opponents spent too much time building engines and not enough time going for points, but the fact that I won the game without much of an engine was a turn-off for me.  I still like the game alright, but it's not one I'll ever ask to play.

RATING: 8
This might be the best Euro-ish game that I have ever played.  This has some similarity to [thing=8217][/thing] and [thing=28143][/thing], only this one is more enjoyable in many ways.  I like San Juan, but wish it was a little more flexible and maybe a little more complicated.  RftG tries to be a great game and tries to fill the gaps in SJ, but fails miserably, because it almost always becomes a race to grab VP chips.  Both games are multi-player solitaire games where the players compare scores.

This game is more interactive: You get the chance to take cards from your opponent.  You also manage the pool of cards in the middle, to keep him from getting cards he needs.  Instead of just being "luck of the draw," there is hand management involved, which creates more player interaction.

There are also buildings that you can build that can potentially change the victory conditions, so while your buddy may be building a zillon VPs, you can play a building, collect all roles in your clientelle, and score the automatic victory.  There is far more complexity and versatility than Race.  It is just a far better game, overall.  If you play RftG, do yourself a favor and upgrade to this.  You won't be disappointed.

RATING: 8
A fascinating, offbeat, indie-developed card game.  Not so professional as RftG, Dominion, or the superb Blue Moon and Jambo.  Lots of very neat (and surprisingly balanced) building powers that keep you coming back.

Unfortunately, over time my initial love has turned into sadness.  The card powers ARE cool, the pool IS a really neat interaction mechanism, and this is (while it lasts) more FUN than RftG or Dominion.  Unfortunately, the scoring system leads to annoying play (and in 2p at least favors whoever happens to draw more blue cards), keeping track of all your own and opponents' powers is a nightmare, the rules are slightly overly complex, etc.  It's flawed enough (ignoring the graphics) to not measure up to its promise, after enough plays to see what's wrong.  Sigh.

RATING: 8.5
Fantastic multi-use card game that's sort of similar to Race for the Galaxy - role selection and cards serving as costs, buildings, and VP in some way shape or form.  I find Race the more elegant game, since it's got less moving parts and the game scales a lot nicer into the endgame (discounts and cardflow + goods for VP rather than giant client-powered actions and cards to vault), but pool control is a really interesting thing that other games of its type don't have.  It plays particularly well as a shorter, heavier game seating 3-5 which is a very nice niche to have.

Update 7/23/14: Revised rating down slightly after playing a pile of games with the Imperium variant and burning through the "new game" impression.  It's a very good game, but the randomness still bothers me a bit - you really need to get certain cards at the right time for a lot of strategies to work, and if people aggressively skip their turns it can lead to people just getting shut out for a while.

Update 8/1/14: Rating revised back up, we were derping on the rules for players skipping their turn.  Ignore my previous comment!

RATING: 8
2-5(4)

UPDATE: I think I like the power combinations and gameflow of Innovation a touch better ... it just seems smoother and more streamlined, despite the chaos ... but there are a lot of interesting combos to build, and a lot of ways to really boost your engine (patrons, merchants, etc.). A craftsman patron does, indeed, seem strong, though.

--------------------------------------------------------

It's pretty hectic and actually takes a lot of table space. I don't think I like it more than Innovation (though the two are substantially different) due to some recordkeeping and how much longer it feels like it plays. However, this has somewhat more strategy than Innovation. It plays longer and is less swingy; you at least know what "broken" building somebody is trying to build several turns before it comes out.

The clientele / piggybacking thing can be severely limiting, but I think that's just a nice part of the strategy.

I just had no enthusiasm for playing anything after playing this.

RATING: 5
From 10000m, this is poker without the betting.  It amounts to getting the best patterns of cards via decisions of draws (admittedly pumping through a deep lifecycle of cards).  The possible player states ('hands') is much much greater than poker, but once a handle on the combos is attained, the decision making is basically just choosing what end state to shoot for given some notion of odds of getting there.

Note that it is the chaos level that degenerates GtR to this level, in spite of the fancy card lifecycle and player interactions.

But this is far far more complex in state than poker.  Which in the end amounts to learning curve to get to the same game.  If you are into discovery with a card game, then this is probably good.  If not, like for me, this is not good.

Finally, poker /does/ have betting as a critical major value add, which GtR does not have.  Hence this is far inferior to poker.

RATING: 8
Innovation launched Carl Chudyk into my favorite designers; Glory to Rome cemented him there. This game is a masterpiece of overloaded components. Cards are actions, bonus actions, buildings, materials, and points. The game is about transforming these cards between those states. That hits all of my buttons.

I'm not particularly good at the strategy to the game yet, and it seems that the game is a bit more amenable to long-term thinking than Innovation.

Oh, and the roles system is a great example of player interaction done right.

RATING: 7
Carl Chudyk manages to make games that feel unbalance yet be balanced at the same time. And Glory to Rome is no exception. This is a card-driven engine-building game where certain combos can quickly lead to insurmountable engines. But through knowing the cards/options of other players, it also manages to feel controllable. And compared to some of his other designs (Innovation, Mottainai), the game allows more time to develop one's engine, which I find quite satisfying. 

Really, the biggest issue with this game is learning about how the game flows and how the cards link/combo together. If you play it once, I can see the game feeling like a chaotic mess. But with multiple play, it really opens up into something quite interesting.

RATING: 9
Complex to grasp at first. Interesting use of cards, as they can represent a role, a building material, a building and money/victory points.

RATING: 6
2011 Mar 23
I played the game 3 times in one day and found the game to be fun enough.  Although, I did win 2 of the 3 games so that colors my feelings towards the game a bit.  I just find that Race for the Galaxy to be superior in terms of balance.  You'll find in Glory to Rome that the powerful cards are no more expensive than cards that are not as great.  I find that due to this, Glory to Rome is much more "swingy" since you can pull of gnarly combos a lot sooner than you can in Race for the Galaxy.  There is a nice build up for Race for the Galaxy compared to Glory to Rome.

RATING: 3
Pre-game impressions weren't good. What seemed to be an overtly complicated card game with garish graphics soon revealled itself to be quite a tactical little game where you had to choose your own approach to victory whilst trying not to benifit your neighbours.

Some buildings do seem extremely powerful and when played in conjunction with others seem unbeatable. Here the early card draw is crucial and the rub of the cards could spin against you but not a bad 60 minutes once you get your head around the rules.

Second Play: The rub of the cards does seem to stear the action and if you don't draw decent buildings then you will be sunk. Early builds and client expansion seems essential to benifit from client follow ons. One more play to be sure but I think there may be far more luck than skill with this one. (Rating reduced)

Third Play: My comments for play 2 are only repeated and emphasised. The rub of the cards drawn especially in the early game utterly sets the success of the game. Its clear that certain players relish the randomness but the experience just strikes me as futile. Time to put this up for trade or sale.

Fourth Play: How did I forget I disliked this so much! Same again, the luck of the card draw utterly dictates what happens. Without clever buildings to build or the materials to build them you are in a vacuum. Sure materials in the vault is the path to victory but if you can't get materials in the stockpile you aren't going anywhere. I can see no strategic opportunities here if you don't get the cards you need. You might as well roll dice. Rating dropped again. Please shoot me if you ever see me at a table with this again.

RATING: 9
I rarely give out perfect scores to games, but this game doesnt get a perfect score either. but its still a great game. In this game you use the bodies of craftsmen, merchants, legionaries etc. to build glorious structures for the glory of rome (see what i did there). you can build such wonders as to rival the works of the great augustus...and still be beaten by a compulsive hoarder of bricks.
 This game can be hard to grasp initially.. i know it was for me and rin and we're basically geniuses so good luck to the rest of you. The key paradigm shift is that people can be buildings and bricks at the same time in a weird superposition of states. basically these are quantum cards. not for small children as you need a grasp of quantum mechanics to play this game.

RATING: 9
Game has a pretty steep learning curve but once it clicks it is just so much fun. The game runs smoothly and the cleverly designed cards facilitate the 'one card represents lots of different things' very well. A lot of the cards seem overpowered when you first see them and really insane combo's can be created. But surprisingly this does not break the game. It just adds to the fun factor. It can be a bit luck dependent but that does not really bother me. Neither does the artwork I've heard so many people complain about. Very clever cardgame that could become a favorite.

RATING: 8
A lot of game in a small package and a short playing time (after that first play). A little like Puerto Rico, with the roles and the buildings with their different powers. But unlike PR, you're forced to take a different approach each time, as you can only work with the cards you get.  SO the luck of the draw becomes a feature, rather than a problem.  Different paths to victory also add to the depth and replayability of the game.

My group has played Glory to Rome more than any other game this year (2006).  Along with Caylus, my favorite game from 2005.

RATING: N/A
BENJ
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Es un juego en el que cada jugador participa en la reconstrucción de roma, después del incencio de Nerón (creo). Es un juego de cartas. Cada jugador tiene un tablero y las cartas se van ubicando a uno u otro lado del tablero. Según su ubicación las cartas tienen una función diferente. Cada carta es a la vez, un personaje, un edificio, un tipo de material, un valor de influencia y un valor de tesoro. 

En cada turno, un jugador juega una carta de personaje (roles). Cada personaje hace una cosa. El resto de los jugadores, pueden decidir si van a jugar el mismo rol, o pasan y simplemente roban cartas (piensan). Se pueden ir acumulando clientes (roles ya jugados que te dan derecho a aprovecharte de roles de otros jugadores sin gastar cartas), metiendo materiales al almacen, consiguiendo tesoros, o construyendo edificios. Los edificios acabados otorgan funciones especiales e influencia. La influencia aumenta el límite de cartas de roles y de tesoro que puedes tener en el tablero. 

El juego es interesante. Es necesario jugar más partidas para valorar con más justicia, pero de momento la primera impresión es muy positiva. Empieza con un 7.5 y quizás vaya subiendo..

Sin duda recomendable. Sin tener mucho que ver.. el juego puede estar a la altura de 7 wonders, por ejemplo.

RATING: 9
Rome demands you play this game!
The card interactions and strategies in this game can be a little complicated and unwieldy at first, and it has a very steep learning curve the first time through. Once you get past that hurdle, however, this is a very elegant and entertaining game with very deep strategy requiring you to not only work on developing your own strategy, but forcing you to keep a close eye on the other players' as well.
The artwork is bright and entertaining, and each card contains a classical quote relating to the card's name and function, which just adds flavour and fun to the game.
Anyway, if you can get past the initial complexity of the game, it's a lot of fun.

RATING: 9
[b]Snowballing card engine:[/b] I feel like we've barely scratched the surface with this one, but it's a keeper. One of those games - like Parade - where you pretty quickly reach the point where someone could potentially end the game at any moment. Now that I think about it, that possibility seems like a crucial balance to Chudyk's crazy cards in that you need to be able to close when you're on the up. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This one really lives up to the hype. Fun, challenging, and surprisingly tense. The strategy element is fantastic--not only are you trying to figure out the best combos, but every action you make will create opportunities for your opponents. You have to be so careful. 

Like its sister game Innovation, there are a lot of really powerful, game-warping effects on these cards. Things can swing back and forth pretty rapidly. There are multiple buildings that can end the game the moment you construct them, for example. It definitely doesn't have quite the chaotic feel of Innovation--cards aren't flying around as much--but it's swingy, no question. I don't mind this. 

The big downside is that this game's art is cartoony and terrible. Apparently there are other editions that don't have this problem. I'm perfectly willing to overlook bad art in a great game. More significantly, there are a few balance issues. A weak opening client, which is simply a matter of luck, can make a game feel lost from the start. The Coliseum is a notably overpowered building, and definitely not in a fun way. (Both these issues are easily fixed by house rule, if you so desire.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The artwork of the Lookout Games version is ok.

My only negative on the components is that the box is too small. Yes, it's true that it's just as big as necessary but if you want to sleeve the cards or fit the expansion with it...nope...not gonna happen.

Let's get to the game itself.
It has a learning curve until you know the cards and start figuring out some good combinations but until then you might be a Little overwhelmed or confused on how to get your empire running.

I really love the multi-use-cards and I've been a fan of that since I first encountered it.

Pretty cool game that has a lot to offer in just a few cards and I'm really looking forward to discovering what else I could find in there.

RATING: 8
For years I avoided this because of the garish art -- and finally picked it up just before the Black Box was released.  Having now played both, I actually prefer the (admittedly garish and silly) early version; it does nothing to hide the marvelous strategic options or cutthroat play.

I love Race for the Galaxy and Eminent Domain, but this is so much more bloodthirsty, and because unlike those games, you use one deck for pretty much everything -- roles, materials (which kind of act as 6 mutually exclusive currencies), and buildings -- and because you have to manage your influence, buildings, clientele, stockpile, and vault, not to mention the pools, you have a dizzying array of options, all of which somehow manage to fall short of what you NEED.

And I feel like I'm just getting started seeing the possibilities.  I needed a card in the pool that wasn't there, and finally realized that I could lead a matching card to get one there.  (Two player game, so not as risky as it would be with more players.)  Of course, by the time I could fetch it back, the situation had changed, and I wound up doing something else entirely.  The endgame was awesome; my opponent stopped the game before I could finish my statue, although I had a ton of finished buildings and he had very few.  But his vault was superior enough to mine to eke out a 1-point win.

I look forward to many more plays of this one.

RATING: 10
The sometimes chaotic but always fun role selection and resource management card game. Players use cards as either roles, buildings, building materials or clients. This leads to some intersting decisions about the best way to use a card. The IV version is sometimes unfairly maligned (IMHO) for it's cartoon style design. Personally I really like this design which is colourful a full of character. I do accept that there are some design flaws which the Black Box version will no doubt address but what flaws there are don't impact gameplay much. Players should be prepared for some card combinations which are overpowered and game-breaking but this isn't MtG and has no pretentions to trying to achieve that level of balance. A fun game which I find I want to play again win or lose.

RATING: 6.5
I traded World Without End, my latest bete noir (at least for 2), for a nice copy of this.  I definitely believe the hype on this one.  We haven't played enough to understand the cards yet, but it is a whole lot more satisfying than RftG, if only because everything you do in Glory to Rome is open.  Not open info, per se, but if you discard, it will be up for grabs to others (including yourself).  The amount of variety for strategy in this one is very deep, and the mechanics, which do take a little while to get used to (although they're really quite easy once you get the hang of it), really make the game shine.  Having the cards be so multi-valent really makes you feel free as a player, with possible magic at your fingertips, while the flow of play locks you into those tough decisions.  This is my new favorite card game (well, at least it is neck and neck with Seaside) and worth paying even $40-50 for.  Great game.

RATING: 7.5
There's a lot here to like - the turn structure is enviably simple and six role choices is far from complicated but the interaction of these with the card options available to you results in a satisfyingly complex game.  The card design is also very well done, with the multiple uses of each card clear and well matched well to the design.  The 'camp' card is equally well designed as a player aid - being all you really need once you've made it through the rules, other than an ocassional reference to the detailed explanation of card descriptions in the annex to the rules.  Having said that the first game went on too long, but that is pretty much inevitable with these types of games - where you need to absorb the info and options on cards etc which effectively add to the rules before you can playing efficiently - and I would expect future games to be shorter as we'll be more familiar with the rules and the cards.  That will also enable us to concentrate more on useful card combinations - those that arose in our first game were probably more accident than design - and add more tension to the finish (as well as answering the question we had as to whether drawing the Forum wins you the game).  A good game and I expect it to improve with subsequent plays.

A few further plays confirms that first impressions were fairly accurate but raises some concerns about the hidden vault scoring being such a major impact on the final score and possibly making it a bit too random (unless you enjoy a level of card counting which is much heavier than the rest of the game really justifies; in which case there's probably a queue of Freudian analysts just itching to see you). I also have a concern that being the first to pick up a Patron client can lead to a bit of a runaway leader problem.  Nevertheless, it remains an interesting design worth playing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
UPDATE: Why all the hate for the art? Yes, it's cheesy. But that blends with the flavor of the game: the out-of-control building powers, the sometimes-vicious player interaction, the light-hearted take on contractor corruption. If this had serious art, you might mistake it for a serious game. I like Glory to Rome, but it just doesn't have the repeat draw of it's peers. After maybe 15 games, it just doesn't draw me anymore, and I think it's a combination of factors: games frequently effectively decided too early with a drawn-out process of one player hammering the rest, a far greater propensity for indivudal mistakes to significantly unbalance the game than even the notorious Puerto Rico, and too many knife's edge decisions where minor mistakes can lead to catastrophe, and therefore a needless sapping of the pace of the game as players diligently try to avoid these mistakes. There really is a lot of fun in the package here. But I think once you hit a certain level of mastery, for most the appeal will fall off dramatically. Still a very fun game until you get to that point though. 
---
UPDATE: After a bunch of play, I've knocked my rating down just a touch. This is still a great and fun game, but I think it can go painfully long from time to time and that the luck factor here is incredibly high. You can bring a lot of skill to bear on the game too, but sometimes you just don't get the cards, and you end up never really going anywhere and not really having fun. San Juan and Race for the Galaxy are games that are doing in the main the same thing, but in a far more polished, elegant, less punishing way. That doesn't mean GtR doesn't have a niche appeal, it clearly does; it's just up against 2 all-time classics. Most days I would easily take SJ or Race first.
---
UPDATE: Not sure my balance concerns are justified. There are a lot of powerful buildings in the deck, and while the Colosseum obviously is powerful, the threat to clients is good to have, it's expensive, and there are other combinations that are also brutal.
---
UPDATE: I do really enjoy this game, but some of the card powers are pretty wild. I'm considering just taking the Colosseum out of the game it's so nasty compared to the other cards (I suppose I should figure out a few underpowered buildings to remove from the other suits to keep the frequency of the roles and materials right). The balance of the building powers doesn't feel great, some of the buildings are killer while some aren't real winners, but it's a chaotic game, so it works out OK. I've been having quite a lot of fun with it. Playtime has definitely come down with experience, although sometime the game seems to run a little longer than it should.

The sweet spot, player-wise, seems to be 4. With 5 the pacing feels a little slow to me.
---
UPDATE: Next couple plays came down closer to 1hr, so that's good. It's fun, but it definitely feels a bit more out-of-control, with 6 different types of cards to draw from, you can feel a bit hosed from time to time as the cards you need just don't show up, which can be a bummer. But we're still enjoying it a lot.
---
So far I quite like it, but  I'm a little worried about the playing time. It says 60 minutes on the box; our last game went 90. I think at 90 minutes it's just a bit too repetitive. 60 would be perfect. Regardless, I really like the combination of stuff in the game and personally look forward to playing again. But I'm not sure that it's gone over well enough with the folks I've played it with to get traction. It's certainly pretty similar to Race for the Galaxy and San Juan, and both those games are far tighter. For me personally, it expands the genre, and I'm glad to have all three. But it may be a while before everyone else has played enough Race to get excited about this one.

Glory to Rome is sort of an expanded and more chaotic San Juan. Now instead of just being able to pick roles and take whatever roles are picked, you have to have the right card to start or get in on the action. And you can't just build buildings by spending any old cards, you have to have the right materials. And the building powers are no longer subtle, but most mid-level buildings tend to be pretty powerful and high-level buildings are game-altering. It's a nice mix, but I think it might  be a little too much, and as a result the learning curve on this one is pretty high. OK, but with a longer playing time and with a greater potential for analysis paralysis, it's harder to get multiple games in a short time, the way it's easy to come up to speed quickly with Race.

Anyway, I quite like the game, but I'm still waiting to see if it's going to fly of if it's going to be another of those games that I like a lot but never get to play.

RATING: 6

It might have just been an anomaly, but it was still rather off-putting how easy they seemed to fulfill.

Overall, I love the system, but am still unsure about game balance and the validity of shooting for end-game scoring.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]Includes The Republic expansion.[/b]
If there's a contest between San Juan, Race for the Galaxy, and Glory to Rome for "best card tableau game," then my vote would be GtR. Glory to Rome is one of the meatier card games that I like to play, and I hope to get yet more plays in to explore even further the card combinations. 

Like Puerto Rico, in Glory to Rome, a player chooses a role, and then everyone follows that role. I find this "follow the leader" mechanic creates a great rhythm to the game, where everyone is constantly involved. What also makes GtR a bit unusual is that many of the cards are wildly powerful, creating combinations that can have dramatic effect on player tableaus. And this is where I suspect increased familiarity will add even more enjoyment as players explore those possibilities.

Regarding the glorified "clip art" appearance of the game, I don't mind. I find it kind of charming and endearing, though I did sleeve my cards for durability. If I stumbled across a black box edition, I might be tempted, but my concern would be that the garish colors of the original are immediately identified in that muck of cards in the middle when it comes to choosing building materials. A more discrete artwork might actually hinder that a bit.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This card game is not as difficult as its reputation suggests. It is accessible though challenging. Each card may be played in one of several different possible ways. Balancing whether a card should be played for its role, its material, its building, or person, makes for some exciting decisions. Yes, there is some luck involved, in that you can only build the building which you are lucky enough to draw which may leave you at a disadvantage to your opponent's draw. Nevertheless, with multiple ways to get on the victory path, the luck is mitigated. I hesitate to suggest that there are multiple paths to victory, because in my experience, there appear to be about two main paths, with one being stronger than the other. But there are many ways of moving yourself down that path. Great game. Very enjoyable.

RATING: 7
While not as enjoyable as Carl Chudyk's Innovation, I still usually enjoy this game.  I think that fans generally agree that the main goal in this game is to find some "broken" combination of cards and exploit that as much as possible.  (What that combination can be very much depends upon the cards you receive during the early course of the game).  

One minor drawback is that the 'craftsman' action very much seems to be better than getting buildings into play via the 'architect' action (involving the extra step of getting cards into your stockpile, although a card like "Sewers" helps you out in this respect).  

So don't take this game too seriously, and enjoy the ride you, or someone else, find(s) to win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My first play of this I was immediately struck by its similarity to Race for the Galaxy.  Several plays later, I continue to think it is similar but I see a lot of differences.

Some similarities are that it involves role selection and cards serve multiple purposes.  If possible, the cards serve even more purposes than they do in RftG.  The differences include more ways of reaching end game conditions, hidden veeps in the form of material in one's vault, and more player interaction.  I think GtR is easier to learn and teach than RftG.  The player mats are very helpful.  

I think RtfG is a tiny bit better for two players.  The Legionary role of GtR suffers with only two.  Even so, more often that not I've played and enjoyed GtR with only 2.

I enjoy both games a great deal.  They're different enough that it's worth owning both.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Played at BGG.Con.  Now, to be fair to this game, I was REALLY, REALLY drunk when I played it.  

Basically, its like San Juan, bulked up on total Steroids. I HATE San Juan, but I liked Glory to Rome.  Lots of little buildings your putting up, lots actions to set up the process of building stuff. I wasn't going to win in the game I played, but I built a fort early (making me immune to the other people stealing my stuff), and focused on just trying to figure out how stuff got done, and managed to get in a position of kingmaker.  Not bad for being really, really drunk.

However, if you're looking for a game ABOUT Rome, and Roman politics, pass this by. This has NOTHING to do about Rome.  Its a good little building game, and is definitely interesting in its own right, but any semblance of a resemblance to political culture of Roman Patricians backing public works in order to gain favor in the political intrigue of the Senate is purely accidental.  This game demonstrates my pet peeve about 'Victory Points'- without some context of what they mean, the game itself is just sort of...pointless. The Patrician class saw these buildings as an ESSENTIAL tool in the competition for prestige and political power in the Senate- but they were still a means to an end. In Glory to Rome, the buildings and the points they give you are the end in themselves- putting the cart before the horse.

But still, a good game, and I'd like to try it again while not so drunk.

Darilian

Experience: Very interesting. The leading/following and clientele mechanisms feel very new to me. In my one playing there was almost always something to do. The game was immediately interesting.

RATING: N/A
Glory to Rome is a card-based city building and resource management game with a novel mechanism. Each card may act as a building, a client, a raw material, or a valuable resource, frequently forcing players into difficult decisions regarding how each card should be used. In addition, much of the game is played from the discard pool, giving players some control over what cards are accessible to opponents. Actions are triggered by a form of card-driven role selection -- the active player leader a role, and other players may follow if they discard a matching card from hand (to the pool). Players who don't follow may 'think' to draw more cards. There are thus strong interactions between the different uses of cards. Scoring is a combination of completing buildings and storing resources, with end-of-game bonuses for storing a diverse assortment. Game length is player-controlled, and is triggered in a few different ways.

RATING: 7
The unknown publisher and relatively mediocre components led to expectations that the game far exceeds.  It's a very worthy addition to the recently crowded Choose Roles & Build Stuff genre.  Just a little heavier and longer than Race For The Galaxy, it features somewhat more player interaction.  There are a few cards that let you actually mess with other people's stuff, and you also have some control over which cards become available to your opponents.  Fans of the genre should definitely give it a shot.  (A bigger publisher should also consider a reprint with thicker cards and perhaps fancier artwork.)

RATING: 7
[2013-07-25 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
It took me a long time to come up with a rating for Glory To Rome (GtR). The first few plays, I couldn't wrap my head around the system and was playing cards almost randomly. After a few  more plays, things started to come together and I did enjoy GtR more and more. Unfortunately, most of my friends don't value GtR that high and so it's always a fight for me to get it to the table. The most heard complaint is that GtR is too fiddly and that the rules are a convoluted mess. While I think that this is true to a certain degree, there's a lot to like once you know how this machine works. 

I would love to explore GtR and it's crazy combos further in the future now that I'm not struggling with the basic mechanisms anymore. If I do get the chance to do so, I will also update my rating accordingly. Until then, this is a solid 7.

RATING: 9
Really good game - quick(ish) strategic and fun and it also plays well with 2 through 5 players. One of the best 'build an engine' games around, with a huge variety to experiment with due to the wide variety of buildings available and the multiple end conditions.

When playing with only 2, some of the buildings which grant 1 think/action per influence upon completion, perhaps should have some 'cap' put on them as this can get a little silly given how much influence you can get in a 2-player game.

Very slightly let down by the production quality - the style of artwork grew on me (I was very negative about it originally - but it sort of fits with a game which has some 'take that' moments) but it would probably still benefit from a less cartoony feel - and linen finish cards always, always benefits a card game.

It's still worth killing your granny to get a copy of one of the existing editions but hopefully it will be reprinted (with some improvements) to avoid such a drastic price.

RATING: 8
Like others have said, this is a lot like San Juan, but with more strategy.  Like any card game, sometimes you don't draw the cards you need.  But its easy enough to switch gears and go in a different direction most of the time.  I can see this getting a lot of play.

The game is good enough that it should really come in a larger box.  The negatives have to do with production/development.  The plastic container it comes in is too small and seems cheap.  The cards are also tightly boxed into 3 card-boxes.  I like the card boxes but they are too small.  It's a pain to put the cards back in there...which as someone noted, is only going to be tougher as the cards get used more.  Oh yeah, and the site cards don't have a box....just an open ended flimsy cellophane "container".  I would have prefer they upgrade the components and charge a little more for the game.  They could even leave out the merchant bonus chips.  They don't seem necessary.

Also, you need a lot of room to play this game.  There are lots are cards scattered about in the play area: All around your playmat, buildings (to the side), and card pool (in the middle)...not to mention space for the drawdeck and jacks.

RATING: 8
Players are rebuilding bits of Rome after the fires of 64AD.  Meaty card game, very much in the vein of San Juan, where players are managing cards through various states (around 5, I think) via the now ubiquitous role selection mechanic.  The initial 15 minutes are a big "huh?", but then it clicks and literally flies along.  The rules sensibly recommend ignoring the building powers for the first game - great move, as the buildings add several layers of depth to the game play.  Good rules, nice if rather colourful presentation.  Several game end triggers to watch out for.  I'm enjoying this one, and it's getting better.

RATING: 9
Idea:
Play cards from hand to activate actions.

Mechanism:
Each card has 3 different "values" a) its color which it activates b) material that is used on building = same as color though c) building effect-

First player plays a card to activate action: a) patron to put card from table aside to activate actions free b) Build from hand or storage c) collect card from table to storage d) fight - reveal card to steal one of that color from neighbors hands e) store card from storage to vault as VPs

Other either join the action by playing identical card (or getting it free if you have color as patron) or draw cards.

Idea is to build buildings and store cards to vault for VPs. Finished buildings also give effects like increase hand size.

to who:
Those who quick strategy card games that still have enough to wash out luck element.

Note: original copy looks neat but I didnt like the new copy's graphics

RATING: 10
Az egyik kedvenc játékom. Ha sikerül felfogni minden szabályt elsőre, akkor egyből egy nagyszerű játékélményt nyújt. Rengeteg olyan mechanizmus van a játékban, ami tetszik, kezdve a kártyalapok többféle felhasználási módjától, a kézlimit átlépésén át, a két ugyanolyan színű lap szenátorként való felhasználásán keresztül, egészen a meditálás utáni ügyfél általi cselekvési lehetőségig (követőként) és még sorolhatnám. A szerencse szerepe végig jelen van (kártyajáték lévén), tehát abból kell gazdálkodni, ami jön. Egy példa a szerencsére. A tegnapi játéknapon a sógorom felépítette a kolosszeumot, ami által légiós szerep választásakor képes volt a szomszédaitól a nyersanyagon kívül ügyfeleket is elszipkázni, ezzel a többszörös akció lehetőséget csökkenteni (nagyon fájdalmas tud lenni :)) és ezzel egyidőben azokat a kincstárába helyezni (természetesen a befolyás korlát figyelembevételével), amik a végén győzelmi pontokat jelentenek. Nagyon erős épület, ami ellen szerencsére lehet védekezni a Fal vagy Palánk megépítésével (az más kérdés, hogy azok meg áthidalhatók egy Híd megépítésével). Hogy jön ide a szerencse szerepe? Hát úgy, hogyha nem sikerül egyáltalán Fal vagy Palánk lapot húzni, akkor teljesen kiszolgáltatott maradsz a Kolosszeummal szemben. Tegnap nekünk szerencsénk volt, mert sikerült elég hamar egy-egy Falat felhúzni (ami a végén más előnyt is biztosított), mialatt a sógoromnak nem sikerült Híd kártyát felhúznia. A játék egy kicsit komplex, de 1 játék után szerintem minden a helyére kerül és utána további játékalkalmak adnak lehetőséget a különböző izgalmas kombinációk (értsd, sok győzelmi pontot hozó) felkutatására, de bőven megéri a ráfordított időt. Dicsőség Rómának! (2015.08.17.)

RATING: 5
I've tried this one multiple times, but it has yet to click with me.  It was sold to me as a building game like RftG, but I don't think it has too many similarities to other such titles.  The silly amount of unintuitive complexity in the card flow rules (where stuff goes if you discard it, or play it, or use it to do an action), plus the oddity of the endgame goals (you're trying to get points, but most of your points come from hoarding resources, not building with them) always frustrates me.  It really feels like 5 functions for each card and a half-dozen different pools that they can shuffle between is just too much fiddliness, and for far too little payoff.
Oh, and the artwork..  The amount of ridiculous snobbery surrounding merely the printing of this game makes me laugh.  The old cartoony art wasn't great, but it was serviceable and I didn't hate it.  The international edition looks generally better, if less quirky.  And the deluxe edition looks like a bad throwback to the 70s.  This minimal style does nothing for me, and I find it baffling that so many people prefer it.  It doesn't hurt my desire to play the game (I'll let the gameplay do that), but still..

RATING: 7
There's urgency each turn to maintain your position in the race. There's tension created by the multiple stages required to make publicly-visible progress when building a building, moving materials to a vault, or using something from the pool. There are several ways to interact, and slow down leaders, which is a very welcome thing in an engine-building race game.

The length is great. The powerful combos are entertaining.

But sometimes you won't draw good cards, and the pool will be empty, and it'll be difficult for you to affect the game. If you find yourself in this situation after someone has built a powerful combo, you've probably already lost. In a 2-player game you can resign. But in a 3-player game, what do you do?

RATING: 6
Glory to Rome is another card game where cards serve multiple functions (ala San Juan and Race for the Galaxy).  The cards convey 4 important pieces of information: (1) resource type, (2) VP value, (3) Associated role action, and (4) Special ability when the card is 'built'.

On your turn you can simply draw card(s) or play an role card to perform it's associated action.  If you play an action other people can likely take part in the initiated action and/or draw card(s).  The game comes with player board to track your influence (dictates number of clientel/vault cards), vault (cards you have played face down for their VP value only), clientel (cards which allow you to take role associated actions even if you don't have the specific role card in your hand) and stockpile (cards for the six type of resources.  The game can end in multiple ways, which are in control of the players.  One of which causes one person to instantly win regardless of individuals VP, so be careful.

Overall, there are a neat combos that are available and the interaction between players is much higher than in San Juan and RftG.  I can see what people meant when they said that the likely winner will be the one that can create the most 'broken' combo first.  Glory to Rome is still definitely worth checking and I'm sure some will even prefer this one, to the more multiplayer solitaire tablue-based card games.

RATING: 9.5
[b][thread=815795]The PLAYMOBIL® Legion reviews "Glory to Rome: Black Box"[/thread][/b]

(As of [b]10[/b] plays) An innovative hand management card game with many different, shifting strategies involved. Quite tricky to get your head around the gameplay at first, and there are almost too many moving parts, but once you're familiar with how everything works it gets less intimidating and presents you with very challenging and fun decisions every turn. Has gradually climbed in my ratings from a 7 to a 9.

The Black Box edition is very elegant and makes it easier to introduce this excellent game to gamers who might otherwise be put off by the cartoonish graphics and gradients of the original. While I am happy to play with the original, I'm much more comfortable pulling this version out.

[b]2 year verdict:[/b] 10 plays in and I'm pretty much hooked. While I was supremely unsure of its merits the first time I played it, it has gradually become my favorite hand management game. It feels like there's still a lot more fun stuff to explore here.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Grudging respect which has grown over more plays.  This is a clever system, but just not my kind of game.  I like hurting other players, and this forces you to decide how much you are willing to help others every time you play a card.  Also, not generally a fan of special powers and combinations, but I'm getting the hang of what's possible.

RATING: 9.5
Card-based city building and resource management game with cartoon artwork set in ancient Rome, but with strong strategic/tactical elements.  The cheap packaging and artwork and little known publisher probably keep it from being the big success that it deserves to be.  Comparable to San Juan and Puerto Rico, along with some MtG elements.  I'm very impressed after about a dozen or more plays - takes a bit to get your head around gameplay if you're figuring out from the rules, but much easier to learn if explained firsthand.  Some mechanics and ideas are somewhat reminiscent of Puerto Rico, but this has additional aspects that I found more appealing on first play.  Play time is about 30 minutes or less a player.  Really, really enjoy this!

See my pictorial review: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/371582

[b]Imperial rules[/b] (original) vs [b]Republic[/b] (revised):
[q]The Republic rules derived from the MIT House Rules, but have some further card revisions to make it more balanced. The Imperial rules are functionally identical to the I.V edition.[/q]
Cards changed in Republic: Circus (Crane), Senate (Tribunal), Colosseum (Domus Aurea), Forum (Forum Romanum)

Republic  is available as an expansion: [gameid=121766]
The revised Republic rules/cards (e.g. only need two matching cards instead of three for a petition) are recommended for an easier experience and new players, they have less take-that but also remove most of the pool control part of the game by flooding it.
What people prefer: [thread=1409120][/thread] and [thread=943474][/thread] and [thread=681491][/thread]
Republic originated as a MIT secret house rule described [url=http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/234570/top-secret-do-not-read]here[/url].
Overview of Imperium vs Republic rule: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/image/1156823
See also: [thread=715128][/thread] (note changed Stairway for both rules)

Made custom deck with Asterix-themed cards - see redesign ideas here: [thread=386132][/thread]

Also owned: [gameid=121766] (English European version only)

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
One of my all-time favorite games even though I'm not particularly good at it, and the best strategic card game out there (OK, along with Race). It's always engaging, compelling, and different. It's a real work of genius the way the cards are used in so many different ways, and the buildings are truly inspired not just staid +1 this or -1 that. 

I absolutely love the crazy building combos available and the different ways to get the VP engine going that this game offers while at the same time offering you the "long bomb" of going for the Forum (which wins the game outright regardless of VPs). One could say that all the buildings in G2R are broken and therein lies the balance. The challenge is to build some kind of engine and get it on the track ASAP.

Oh, and for the record, the original artwork never bothered me, and I look down my nose at the people who looked down their noses and wouldn't touch this game until the new version came out. I absolutely loathe the new art as I find its constrained style dull and antithetical to the spirit of the game. But nerd prejudices aside, it's a great game, and everybody should rate it a 10.

RATING: 9
GtR is a great game with lots going on and multiple paths to victory.  It is in the same genre as Race for the Galaxy and less so San Juan in that there is a long and slow, but fortunately not too steep, learning curve, although the gameplay is not the same as these others.  The first hurdle, like RfG, is understanding the game mechanics, which are not terribly intuitive at the start.  As one starts to understand the card powers and combos, and begins to see how to use the mechanics to play off of your opponent(s) leads, things start clicking.  There is lots of stuff going on here, and GtR seems therefore deeper than San Juan and on par with Race.

I really enjoy this game so far, and my wife seems to love it, so I look forward to many more plays.

Addendum: I just uppped my rating from 7.5 to 9 after 38 plays, mostly two player with my wife.  This game has so much to offer.  Every game plays out differently, and I am still finding new interactions and nuances.  Great stuff here!

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.4
2011 TH

Goofball illustrations lead me to believe this card game was going to be a very light party game. What a shock when I discovered this is a strategic game, and one I like, a lot!

The thrust of the game is to rebuild Rome from lowly latrines to exalted temples, and everything in between. When buildings are completed, special abilities are bestowed that combine in surprising and delightful ways with other buildings you have completed, and combinations are endless. (However, the instant win conditions seem too strong--all games won so far have been with the Forum.) 

The cards are of great variety and well designed, and I enjoy the numerous ways each card can be used--very efficient. Lots of decisions. The game plays in 30-45 mins., but can occasionally slow up. Rules are easy. Play is interactive. Replay-ability is high. 

This card game is on the light side of a genre I enjoy (it is often compared to San Juan and the elegant Race for the Galaxy--one of my top 10), but easier to learn and just fun to play. The rating will go up if the Forum ability is not as overpowering as it first appears. This game will get more plays--it is already headed for the next game night.

RATING: 8.5
My favorite card game so far, above [thing=28143][/thing] and [thing=63888][/thing].

I like this thematic blend of mechanics and buildings. A lot of decisions and choices to make according not only your strategy but also according to your fellow opponents's tableau :

(1) - what role to lead or not given other players's clients ?
(2) - what other players's buildings will impact your game or the end game conditions ?

The heart of the game is the pool management. How to recycle the cards in your hand into the pool in order to :
(1) - recruit the clients you want
(2) - take the raw materials for your buildings or your vault

The Black Box edition bring a higher level of gaming experience because of the way the cards convey information with clarity and the use of appropriate colours.

Best with 3 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game feels different each time and is really variable, yet all those crazy special abilities are anchored in really fun core mechanics that ensure a consistently fun experience. 

Don't worry about balance in this game. Each building is ridiculously overpowered in its own way. Keep an eye on what your opponents are building so you aren't caught totally unawares and have your merchants fed to the lions, but mostly build your own awesome strategy. The game is different with 3 and four players (haven't played with 5). With 3 you have time to build a lot more buildings and can get crazier combos. 4 player is a tighter game and you may only get 2 or 3 finished buildings. 

The flowchart on the back of the player "camps" is hilarious. It's a running joke to turn it over and tell new players that this pretty much explains everything so we can go ahead and start. There are like a dozen lines criss-crossing over one another. It does help make sense of the rules so I appreciate it in the end.

RATING: N/A
[i]Oct 2012[/i]: Rating of 8. A very good game, in my top 30.
[i]August 2011:[/i] First Impressions. 9 out of 10.
Fantastic Game! A real gem of a game. I like the card drafting mechanic of being able to choose materials in the pool. The drawings of objects and scenery are good, but the drawings of people are too... whimsical to my eye.  HOWEVER, compelling game play and strategic depth is where Glory to Rome truly shines.

The rules in the booklet are clear and, I found, easy to follow. I had no problems with the game rules after the first couple of games. (Minor quibble: I was disappointed to see glaring typo of a reviewers name. Really, if you're going to bother to add endorsements to your booklet, make sure you get the reviewers name right. This of course, does not affect game play.)

While good with three or more players, I think Glory to Rome isn't as interesting with two players.

The card quality is very good. I won't bother sleeving these cards as the game is relatively inexpensive. The clamshell packaging isn't the best for stacking but it does an ok job of holding the tuckboxes, player mat and rules. The binding of the rules is horrible (one staple? Really?).

Despite the minor quibbles about packaging and art, this game deserves a 9. We'll see if this rating holds up to the test of time.

RATING: 7
Glory To Rome is a classic card game that is pretty screwy and wild but still has a thick covering of strategy to it. Of course what makes it an interesting game is that each card can be used in one of several ways, and knowing which cards to play for what powers at the right time makes it quite interesting. The game develops slowly but as players gain wild powers in goes of the hinges and fortunately ends before it blows up in everyone's face.  While the way cards can be played was certainly novel for its time and is still interesting, the power combos are kind of annoying and it's certainly not worth the $250 people are willing to pay for the "black box" edition.  However, it is a game that I've enjoyed playing. I wouldn't say it's the second coming of card games but it's an important game nonetheless and one everyone should play at least once. I do wish there were actually fewer combos. The game feels like it was designed to be a decent strategy game but then devolve into a bit of a laugh as everyone has a combination of "game breaking" buildings that prove hilarious when activated but sort of cheapen the meaty-ness of the core mechanics. With a different approach to how building effects work, this could have ended up being one of my favourite card games. Instead, it feels too chaotic and wild to make me want to delve into it that much.

RATING: 7
Glory to Rome is a solid game, with a similar architecture to San Juan and Race for the Galaxy.  The game play is a little more chaotic, but there are more levels it seems to each decision.  However, with that said the added chaos in this reduces the over all enjoyment for me.  Being forced to possess a card each time an action is taken in order to accomplish that action leans Glory to Rome on the slightly too much luck for time/weight ratio with me.  Poor buildings may come one's way in San Juan, however the system is designed to at least provide opportunities regardless of you present ability - and yes this will ultimately favor those that get better draws.  However, in Glory to Rome, unlike a San Juan or RftG I found myself unable to accomplish anything early in the game because I kept drawing only one type of card.  There are options here to solve this, but all in all I found it to be a slightly less elegant design.  I also felt it outlasted it's time at the table - lending itself to that repetitious feeling.  This however could be a result of a 5 player affair.  Will play again, and my opinion could easily sway either way.  Do I think it worth owning having San Juan, and RftG in my collection? probably not, but time and more plays will tell.

UPDATE:  This is actually a very interesting game, with a lot more to think about than originally considered.  This game might actually become more favored than RftG or San Juan

RATING: 8
The only problem with this game is that at first glance it appears to be another multi-player solitaire engine building game. The reason that's a problem is that people looking for that kind of experience can get upset when the game is ended unexpectedly by another player, especially through a win by using a Forum.

The game actually requires a great deal more interactivity than is at first apparent, it's just that it tends to be less direct (although there is direct interactivity with Legionnaires and cards like Prison), and more indirect through control of the pool.

Update: This one is moving up in my rankings as I get further plays in.

RATING: 9

I personally feel like the luck factor is large in this one but there are always options, always something you can do to further your position. Hard to explain, but just know this game is a real gamer's game, if you're deep into board games this will satisfy that itch for something new.

RATING: 7
After just a couple of plays, seems like an interesting game. Of course I'm behind the curve (May 2012) and so much has already been written, I'm not going to add anything.

Only further plays will tell if the strategies and play are up to that of Race for the Galaxy. 

Definitely dislike the memory aspect of the Vaults, especially when the scores are so swingy -- huge differences based on remembering correctly what other people put in there. Compare that to a Knizia game that has hidden scoring, like Tigris or Samurai, where the Knizia scoring is much less swingy and unlikely to decide the game if you try to remember everyone else's score. Here, remembering what cards people put it is so vital, because one single card is generally worth 5 points, which is a good 20% of your final score.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
When I first saw photos of Glory to Rome, I immediately dismissed it without even investigating further. I'm sure I wasn't the only one. The garish "Saturday morning cartoon" art style gave the impression of an amateur effort in the super-lightweight category. It looked like a bush league game that almost certainly sucked.

But first impressions can be wrong. Once I finally tried Glory to Rome, I was amazed to find a meaty and nuanced game. It's a game about managing player-triggered communal actions, in the same family as Race for the Galaxy and San Juan. But those games have a more structured feel. Glory to Rome is a wild ride, with avalanches of cards and points, sudden turns of fate, lots of player interaction, and surprises around every corner. The game often feels like it's on the verge of spinning out of control, but it never quite does.

That looseness is the game's strength, but it also means that Glory to Rome is not a game for everyone. Do you look primarily for balance and elegance? Look elsewhere. Do you enjoy managing chaos and adapting to quick fluctuations? Maybe this is the game for you. Be sure to get the I.V edition, which has some important tweaks to improve play balance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Has the wonderful strong powers of CE, but available to all players, in a San Juan style cards do everything format. The pool is a clever mechanism whereby the state of the game determines what clients and materials are available, and which changes to reflect the different actions chosed at different stages in the game.

Good tension between short and long term planning. Too much opportunistic play will stuff up accomplishing longer term goals.

Plays well with 2. I was surprised. Like it 5 player best.

RATING: 8
Man, I really wanted to love playing this game as much as I love its design. Hard to teach, all the different actions and card powers becomes overwhelming for newcomers. Making the best possible combination is a blast, but playing someone who does it is very annoying to me. Game is decided at 70% of its length, and the remaining of the match becomes a rich-gets-richer spectacle.

I can see the appeal, but all the factors aforementioned don't help to bring this one to the table too often.

--------------
[i]Update 06/2012:[/i] Play it only if you want a really brain-burning experience. Casual playing doesn't work here. The rich-gets-richer factor must be controlled by all players, who need to avoid helping each other at all costs. 

About the hard-to-teach factor: just teach it to smart friends. If they don't get it at first then it might not be worth the effort.

RATING: 6
The game for people who feel that Race for the Galaxy is just too simple.  The biggest difference it seems is that the "flow" of cards is a lot more complex in this game versus Race.  In Race, you produce goods and you sell them; in Glory there are more things that a given card can mean, and more potential actions to perform on each player's turn. RATING: 10
Two things make Glory to Rome a 10 for me.

1. When played with seasoned players, it is a consistently tense and rewarding game.  Almost 100% of the decisions you make are seriously important, and it feels that way.  But more importantly ...

2. I continue to learn new things when playing, even after dozens of plays.  The game is deep because the meaning of each card play, and the utility of each building, depends massively on the entire context of play---not only on what's on your own board, but also what's on everyone else's.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I certainly liked it, but I'll need more plays before I've got a good handle on it. I like the multi-use cards and the multiple paths to victory. I think there's a lot of room for variety both in gameplay and strategy. I'm a fan of Innovation, so I expect my rating of this will climb with more plays.

UPDATE: Got the new "black box" edition.
I don't don't know if there is a term to describe this type of game, although I think I've heard "tableau" mentioned before. In essence, this is a complex variant on Rummy. Players have hands of cards that they must play, add to, and transmute to turn them into actions, resources, and victory points. The rules can seem overwhelming at first, but they quickly gel and become second nature. In this way, it also reminds me of [thing=11][/thing]; another game with multi-purpose cards and initially counter-intuitive rules.

The cards offer a variety of play options and combinations so that no two games play the same. Some cards are designed to speed up or immediately end the game, so you can never be sure how much time you have. I read one comment in the forums declaring that the game is "a race to see who can break it the fastest," which seems pretty accurate. 

Overall, a really fantastic and fun game.

RATING: 8
Glory to Rome is a game that meets its reputation.  Each card offers lots of options and the lead/follow mechanic forces each player to pay close attention to what their opponents are doing.  

The obvious downside to the game is the artwork; the original IV edition is poorly illustrated with garish colors and the Black Box edition is overly simplified to the point that the cards are difficult to distinguish from one another.  The European art, on the other hand, is stylishly cartoony and works well for the game.  If an English reprint ever happens (HA!) I hope they can use the Polish/German artwork.

This is a fantastic game.  It is not a $200 game as the current market price suggests, but it is a fantastic game.

RATING: 7
This is a deep card game that throws in an element of Fluxx - your perfectly built tableau can be worth nought if you fail to see another player sneaking to a different victory condition. The limited options here work, as you soon work out which combos to keep an eye out for and how you should try and move towards your own victory. I've downgraded it over time, as too many things have to fall into place for it to be satisfying - I've had more bad games than good ones, simply down to the massive randomness. You also have to rely on other players playing well, which is never something I like in a game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Don't know how we never heard of this game before!  It's vaguely akin to San Juan and Race for the Galaxy, in that you use a card either for its role, or it's building, or its building material.  It takes a game or 2 to understand the rules and know what cards are available, which is not nearly as bad as RftG, which I still can barely figure out!

Some of the buildings are stunningly powerful, like the one that gives you +4 cards, or +2 clients, or allows you to steal from all other players (not just neighbours), and steal from their hands *and* their stockpiles!  The key to the game is usually having enough points to put several valuable cards in your vault, probably getting +3 bonuses for pluralities.  A laborer/merchant strategy could work, although merchants are rare (because they are also stone).  But I guess a craftsman client strategy is better, because the building materials (in your hand) are not so dependent on what other players play.  Legionary is a very useful thing to do to get valuable cards out of people's hands and into your stockpile, especially with the building that allows you to steal a client from other players and put it in your vault!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Of the multiple role-selection, multiple use card games out there, this one is my favorite. It has a fair deal of intricacies, as well as opportunities for plays that enhance your abilities in the future, as well as possibilities for interfering in your opponents' plans (the later here really differentiates this game from it's peers). The lead/follow mechanism is also very well implemented: you always have options at your disposal (and the choices aren't always simple). While the 2 player game isn't as tense as Chudyk's Innovation, it still holds up relatively well when compared to San Juan.

Also worth noting, the CFG Black Box edition of game is beautiful as well as very functional -- a triumph in aesthetic design.

After i fount an introducing video on youtube about Glory to Rome. I immediately got really exited with the game. I already played it more than 200 times and im planning to play it more. This is my and even the group number 1 favorite game so far. I rode so many forum topics of how to win, and the group evolved really well. This game became like chess: if you ask for this i must ask for this to prevent that and every member of the group play like this even in co-op if this is the only way to prevent someone to win with catacoms. While firstly i tought it can be a bit unbalanced but it's not true. I think the game is about to find a great combination of buildings as fast as possibble, like a race. 

Pros / Cons:
-I really like the mechanics of the game, as a card can be used like 5 ways and this also gives the deepness of it.
-Due to the clever mechanics there is very low or absolutely no downtime, you are always involved with the role selections.
-It has huge replayability there are never ever two games the same or similar.
-I love the theme to build the burned Rome.
-The hungarian artwork is outstanding.
-And as the cards flows i feel like the luckfactor in the game is on the lower side which is a big plus.
-It plays around 1-1,5 hour with 4-5 players, and not as preferred with 2.
-The best price / value game so far, in hungary it is with Carcassonne price tag.

RATING: 5
Cards that can mean one of four things--that made my first game somewhat confusing, but I was resigned to losing, and losing badly. However, I came in 2nd! The cards denote one of six roles you can play (like in Puerto Rico), six building materials, or an assortment of victory points. There's definitely a path (sort of like Container) of getting pool cards into your stockpile, which can then be used to build your buildings, or stock your vault with gold, or to get the cards from the deck into your hand, and then into your buildings, or from the pool into your clientele. Confusing? Yes, initially, but it makes sense after a period of disorientation. I'm put off by that, but it looks like a puzzle worth trying to solve. Let's try it again soon...

Update (03/10/09)- 2nd play somewhat less confusing. Still came in 3rd in a 3p game. Game was quicker, but still the mechanics are confusing, as I was admonished for messing up my plays. Oh well. One more time...

Update (03/13/09)- 3rd play. Still mastering the flow of play... the architect and craftsman are similar but act differently! Getting better. Still got crushed--last place in a 5p game.

Update (06/12/09)- 4th play. Have mastered the confusion of the architect and craftsman roles. Built lots of buildings, by getting several craftsman as clienteles early, and getting key buildings set up for 7 card hands, and doubling of clientele actions. However, still lost in a 5p game to the player that managed to squirrel away tons of patron cards in the vault for 35 pts! Ironically, three of us tied at 21 pts. Still working on this.

Update (06/17/09)- 5th play. Finally! Quietly collected six different clienteles and then suddenly completed my Forum for the win! Having the Aqueduct definitely helped. It seems vitally important to build key buildings to help you win--not just any old building.

Update (01/20/12): My initial rating was a '7'. Upon a long reflection, it is a '5'.

RATING: 8
After a single play of the Black Box Edition:

I love tableau games with multi-use cards ([gameid=8217], [gameid=28143], etc.) so this one's right up my blood & vomit-spewed alley... with a really nice statue of Bacchus at the end.  Of course, it's mostly made of rubble, but who's to know but the architect and laborers?  And I had them all, uh, "exiled".

I've always been drawn to the theme and play style of [i]Glory[/i] while being successfully repelled by the hideous clip-art/cartoon "artwork".  The Black Box Edition is absolutely gorgeous, with a very hip mid-century modern graphic design vibe.  And oh, man, can I dig it.

Ordered immediately after first contact and practically vibrating with excitement to see how my regular group takes to it.

[b]After 6 plays:[/b]  Would that I could be but the Rubble Baron, for at least that has a whiff of the Patrician to it.  But alas, lying here alongside a stout and world-wending road without thumbs and sore where the boots have found me I am possessed of nothing but the fact that every road needs a ditch—these things are necessary, and I am clogging up the works.

THIS GAME IS AS BROKEN AS MY SPIRIT

[b]After 15 plays:[/b]  A wonderfully unbalanced [i]exquisite corpse[/i] of a game—we'll be playing this forever...

(All plays are with the [thing=121766]Republic Expansion[/thing].)

RATING: 8
This game would have done better - 8 or 9 if it wasn't for some of the unbalanced end game.  In the games I played the winner always used the 1 building of each color to win.

If all the players know this they can rush out and try to make sure they get this card.  Unfortunately it is very difficult to win otherwise in my experience.

The game has a great mechanic and rewards different styles of play: hold all your cards and control the center pot, or play as fast as you can to get the refill hand draw to maximize the amount of cards you get.

Unfortunately the cards are very busy.  They make good use of card economy having them represent different possible uses, unfortunately that makes them full of information that may not be applicable.  You read a lot about a card when it may ultimately only matter in the color of the card.
