RATING: 8
Very solid system.  Many different paths to take.  Much like many of these kinds of card games what you draw will determine much of what "strategy" you will take, but I feel that the number of choices were plentiful.  

For some reason, I found the free actions initially difficult to absorb, but once you get the game you pick it up quick.  

The art is awful, but there was so many images necessary to making this that I mostly forgive it.  While this certainly has the same kind of thing going as the other Puerto Rico style card games have, there is a lot more going on here that isn't in the other games.  

All and all a winner.

RATING: 8
Really fun game if you like cards that each do many things. There is a lot going on, but it all fits together well after a few plays. 

My only complaint is that some of the powers the buildings bestow are hella powerful. Now, many of them are like that, especially in combination with other cards, so any player has the same chance of getting them, but this does cause the game to be somewhat swingy at times. As a good example of this, in a five player game once we all actually surrendered to a player who had a crazy powerful combo going on - we stopped the game and conceded that there was no way to catch him. It actually states in the rules that surrender is one way the game can end, so just remember that this kind of thing can happen. 

Fun game, but I do think some other card games seem more refined and flow better, such as race for the galaxy. I also prefer Eminent Domain over this one, but I still keep them all. Oh - and probably Innovation too - but only as a two or three player.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
It's sort of like San Juan, but much more complicated. Players will be taking a variety of roles that allow you gather material (wood, rubble, concrete, marble, brick, stone), start/work on buildings, attract clients (who allow you to take these roles more than once a turn), attack their neighbors (demand material), and sell resources for money (VP). When the start player chooses a role (playing a card from their hand), others in turn can either follow (play same color card) or "think" -- draw up to hand limit (5), Jack (wild card for roles), or 1 card if already at hand limit. Then, starting with start player, resolve the roles. Pass the Leader (start player) card.

Every card (6 colors) has multiple functions:
- Role: corresponds to color, and is written on the left.
- Material: corresponds to color, and shown at bottom
- Influence: modifies how well you can do things; shown on top
- Building: description at center (usually gives additional power)
There's a unique mechanism where there are a pool of cards in the center of the table. After a role has been chosen and used, those cards are added to the pool. Roles allow you to move cards around: from pool to stockpile (under player mat), from stockpile to vault (right of player mat), from hand to stockpile or building sites (next to player mat), from pool to client area (left of player mat). When you start a building, you take the card and add it to a foundation card (taken from supply); this indicates how much material you need to complete the building. When it's done, the site card is flipped and moved to top of player mat -- shows influence (and VP). 

There's more -- off site locations, bonuses for types of cards in vault -- but that's the gist.

The player mat takes some getting used to, but is actually quite a clever system. Having a pool of cards is also nice -- even if you don't draw what you need, you'll have access to cards that other players draw and use.

I may have to get this game, although with Race for the Galaxy (which also shares some mechanisms with SJ, but is also more complicated and interesting) coming soon I'm not positive ....
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
This game seems, to me, like a deeper version of Race for the Galaxy.  Don't get me wrong, I still like Race for the Galaxy way more.  It has more variety, better theme, better art, and just goes down a lot smoother.  But this is a great game as well with a lot more depth and harder decisions.

The follow-the-leader mechanic in this game takes on a slightly new twist from others like Puerto Rico and RftG.  Instead of leading with a role (whether you are the only one who can choose it or not) and getting a bonus while others get a weaker version of that action, in Glory to Rome the leader and all his followers get to take the same action (sometimes a follower can even do more depending on his clientele).  The twist is that depending on what your opponents have in their hand they may not be able to do anything.

In this game you build a kind of tableau of Rome (another similarity to RftG) and every card can be one of 5 different things in the game (a client, a resource, influence, Victory Points, and an action card).  Knowing when and how to use them offers a huge amount of depth on how to play the game.  I wish there was more variety to the cards but that is just me being greedy, there's enough here to keep you interested for a long time.

I also have the Black Box edition so I wasn't subjected to the horrible clip-art-laden color vomit of the original.  The new art is very stylish.  It's not my favorite thing in the world, but it looks nice.  Unfortunately the black ink they used for the backs of the cards and covers of the rulebook rubs off on EVERYTHING.  I sleeved my cards so it's not as bad but could they really not use a better printing method besides what I assume must be chimney soot?

Despite how good this game is and how much I enjoy it I may end up getting rid of it.  There just doesn't seem to be any room in my game group's hearts for this one with RftG so good at everything this game does.

RATING: 10
Holy cow!  This is quite honestly the best game of I've played in a long time.  It is a gazillion times better than 7 Wonders...in fact I wonder if 7 Wonders will come out again.  GtR is longer, has a better arc, and has a great rolling power curve which you get to actually keep playing after your powerful combinations come out.  So many game end right as your carefully crafted combos begin to work, but with GtR you actually get to play and grow those combos even bigger.  It's fresh, with tricky decisions at every turn, a play time that is neither too short, nor does it outstay its welcome--in fact right about the time you think someone has solidly taken the lead, the game ends.  I look forward to many many more plays of this one, it's absolutely brilliant!

A brutal knife-fight of a two-player game--oddly reminds me of Aton in that with 2, you are constantly watching the point totals, and trying to keep in mind whats been moved to the Vault--still though right when youre ready to hammer the game to a finish, your opponent pulls out the necessary VPs to make the ending uncertain, or a certain loss.

RATING: 6.5
This is a "first impression" rating after a single 2-player "learning" game in which we played with all the building abilities.  I have to remark first of all what a clever game this really is!  The game design is quite amazing, and even the visual design isn't as bad as popular opinion seems to state.  Sure, the artwork is cheesy, but there's something to be said for clarity.  I saw one re-theme that had much cooler images, but it was much harder to make out all the different elements on the cards, which isn't a problem with the original cards.  As for our 2-player game, it was a bit of a blowout.  I won a "Forum" victory, but I was also way ahead in victory points thanks to a powerful Wall -> Sewer -> Foundry combo.  It makes me think that perhaps this game is not at it's best with 2.  With future 2-player games, I think I'll remove the Catacombs, and instead of the Forum being an automatic win, say that it provides the player with 3 VPs and ends the game if the player has the Forum and one of each client.  Of course, with more knowledge of the many different buildings, it will perhaps be easier to counter your opponent's building combos.  Overall, it was an interesting game that lacked a bit in the "fun" department, perhaps because there was so much to think about with the first play.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 2.5/5  Total: 6.5/10

RATING: 7
[Cambrige Games 2007 I.V edition] {Ancient; hand-management, card-drafting; optimal 4p}  GR2 is an amazing game: full of tactical options and player interaction.  It's difficult to teach G2R effectively esp. when a card servers several functions.  A clearly multifaceted game - there are different ways to get score and players can create their own strategies for winning.

Certain card combo is quite strong but I don't find any balance issue in this game.  However, in my first impression, the luck factor is higher than necessary (in this regard, I prefer Race for the Galaxy).  Esp. if a player has several good building at early game, they could win the game easily.

The production quality is so so.  The cardstock is too thin for durable play.  However, I like the card illustration.

[Variant] The Petition and Forum is a good start for beginners; however, I think it can be dropped when players are more experienced with the game.

RATING: 6
Some people in my group love this game.  I find it pretty much an all-around apathetic "meh" of an experience.  The production quality is mediocre; I don't find the art as eye-gougingly painful as some of my compatriots, but I don't think it's good by any means, and the cards are extremely busy.  This is the second recent game I've played with the giant chaotic discard pile mechanism (Mwahaha! being the other), and I find it somewhat distasteful.  The mechanics are incredibly counterintuitive, and while I find some slight amusement in how incredibly overpowered nearly all the cards seem to be, I find that in practice it doesn't mesh well. (Like To Court the King, I sort of feel like the game resulted from a bunch of guys just sitting around going "You know what would be AWESOME?  If there was a card that did this, but on CRACK!" with little regard to balance or flow.)  

There are some little bits of genius in the game; I think the four-sided cards that function in four different ways depending on how they're placed under your mat are inventive, but the way that this is displayed is too busy and hard to make out.  I'll play this when others want to, but only because it's short enough that I find it just a mild timewaster, rather than an exercise in frustration.

RATING: 9
One of the finest card games ever made. It's an enormously strategic affair as you attempt to erect a variety of buildings which in turn provide various benefits to you once completed and also score victory points. The cards are multi-purpose and can be used as the buildings themselves, building materials or professions that aid you in your attempt to progress through the game. Due to the enormous variety provided by the cards it means that the decisions you make become an intriguing balancing act as you try to optimise the most productive way of selecting/playing them. The artwork looks as though it was taken from a children's history book, and it can be a fiddly game to play as you try to fit numerous groups of cards under your play area, but apart from that it's a fantastic experience.

RATING: 8
tl;dr: great but you have to live with the luck

This game is sort of a predecessor of my all time favorite game: [thing=28143][/thing]. OK, that game was build on the foundations of [thing=8217][/thing], but it did one main thing very different: the huge amount of different, unique cards. Glory to Rome also has that, and cards that can be used in a lot of different ways. It allows players to explore a ton of different strategies over a big amount of games. Of course, just like in RftG you have to work with the cards you get and adapt your strategy accordingly. So there is a certain luck factor that you have to live with. And the buildings that can end the game early can be annoying for other players, but that's easily fixed.
I have the 4th edition that comes with the flashy colors. It's ugly as hell but it's also extremely functional. I don't think I've ever played the black box edition. It looks nicer but I'm not sure it's as clear.
In any case: good, exciting game!

Rating: 8/10
BGG scale: 8/10

RATING: 8
What a novel card game. When I first heard of if, I thought, "Oh, just another game that cleverly uses cards for different things." but now that I've played it, I find it more than that. Despite the bad art, GtR has several great mechanics working together to create a fantastic game. The ever-evolving Card Pool, the useful Thinking, the slow-and-steady Construction, the Patrons for extra Actions, the Legionnaire for stealing cards from other players. Even if they didn't re-use cards for different purposes, this would have been an interesting game. My only concern is that there seem to be a lot of cards that can potentially be over-powered, and this game could possibly end up to be a game of "whoever who draws the best card wins" type. I think I'll wait a bit before acquiring it for my own.

29-Mar-10: Borrowed this from Gregor last Tues, and taught Cynthia how to play on Sunday. We played 5 games of this afterwards! Cynthia is impressed by the sheer number of Options this game presents to the player. I think this is now a definite Must Buy for us now.

RATING: 6
Considering how much I play games, I actually knew very little about Glory to Rome.  I saw it on the table once and was completely turned off by the art style.  Now having played it, I still think the art is terrible, but at least the game play is half decent.

It uses mechanisms similar to [thing=3076][/thing] where each player takes an action, then the other players have a chance to follow the actions.  Except, everything felt clunky to me.  It was like they started from Puerto Rico (which is a game I love) - or probably more accurately, San Juan - and added an expansion that made the game worse.  I could see how people thought this was a great game in 2005.  But having come to it new in 2017, if it were released today the game would be a dud.

RATING: 7.9
Fantastic game. So many choices to pick from at every stage, and so much strategic depth. The game goes a little crazy when the really powerful cards come out, towards the mid- to end-game, but that's part of the charm. The painfully ugly card designs have grown on me a little over time; at least they're fun and colourful. I would have traded up to the Black Box version, but although its designs are certainly elegant and beautiful, they just don't look to me like they suit the game.

I'm looking forward to exploring the potential strategies with more plays of this, and particularly looking forward to trying out the expansions that I've just purchased.

Edit: It seems just about everyone but me hates this in our group, so I doubt it will ever see the table again. It seems to be the crazy endings that put them off. They seem unable to see that you have to play the main part of the game well in order to even reach the stage where "the wheels fall off" (as they put it) first and win the game.

RATING: 8.5
Excellent game. Card art has done the publisher no favours as commented many times, but its format is very well thought out easing what could be a very tangled game into a very slick one. I always loved San Juan, and Race for the Galaxy seemed as though it were the natural successor, but I although I appreciate that was a better game I found it too dry. Just didn't seem to have the same fun element of SJ. Like SJ's eldest son that went into accountancy. Then I played Citadels and that was a whole load worse. SJ's mentally deranged offspring that  sat rocking in a corner, pulling the legs off spiders. But then was born Glory to Rome the true success story of the SJ siblings! Better than its parent in every way I think - a big statement to amke on its first play but I think that will hold up. Played with 5, and there is a fairly quick learning of what you should be doing, although some of the chain effects of buildings you can only grasp with some experience under your belt. Gameplay is essentially the same as SJ but with a few extras. Same kind of perfect time frame for a card game of this nature. Highly recommended.

RATING: 10
Amazing card game in the same vein as Race for the Galaxy and San Juan, but it is, at the same time, more complex and less serious. This is the best of the bunch despite the cartoony artwork. A lot of strategic options are available and there are some really powerful building combinations that can lead to some crazy high scores. The Forum and Catacombs add nice alternative endings, which can alter one's strategy. I've played this mostly with two players and a few times with 3 and once with 4. All numbers are fun since everybody pretty much gets to act during everyone else's turn. I would imagine 5 is a good number too. This is currently one of my favorite games and imagine playing this for a long time to come. I will probably have to buy another copy since my cards are starting to show some wear after 17 plays. Well worth the price though.

RATING: 9
I love the crazy combos and the timing problems that are presented in this game. Up in the air with playing with the MIT secret rule and regular. 2 petition change definitely helps even out the Craftsman and Architect, instead of relying on the players to balance out the Craftsman.

Update: I prefer MIT rules, as you have more options. It also balances the craftsman card flow advantage and buffs the other roles by making the card pool larger.

Rules used: Played with MIT Secret Rules
2 matching roles for petition.
Circus is lead or follow architect with any card.
Forum needs 6 clients and 6 stockpile.

Update: Own black box edition now. Republica rules preferred.

RATING: 4
Rebuilding ancient Rome while skimming off the top to create your own personal fortune. Cards are used as patrons (i.e. what action you take), clients, buildings, building materials, and points. The game centers around constructing a powerful combination of buildings and trying to fill your personal vault with as many valuable materials as possible before the game ends.

Sometimes compared to Race for the Galaxy (cards with multiple uses, role selection, building cards with special powers) but I find that it's not quite as deep in terms of variety of strategies. Feels very reactive, hard to plan.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I have played the two games that this is most often compared to (Race for the Galaxy and San Juan), and after two playings, this is clearly the better game.  It is also clearly the more complex, more involved, and more layered game of the three.  There are an incredible amount of ways to win the game, and each one has a foil.  At first, several buildings seem entirely too powerful, though, upon further consideration, they all have strategies that can easily counter them.  This might be the most enjoyable card based game that I own, trumping Race for the Galaxy by no small margin.

UPDATE:  C'mon, this is one heck of a game.  I cannot believe that thousands here froth at the mouth for Race, while this dude toils in obscurity.  Race is OK (the expansion does stick a bit of interaction into the game), but man, GtR is great.  They are two completely different games, Race and GtR, but I personally love Glory to Rome by a landslide over Race.  That's just me.  But I am right.

RATING: 8
Not the easiest game to learn, but once you're in the Pool, man, it's good.  The amount of killer combos almost cancel each other out and create a balanced game.  I don't think anyone I know - myself included - is "good" at this game yet, but I'm certain it's a game that rewards skillful, clever play.  It's a lot of fun.

As for the art -- a lot of it is fine!  The bright colors make it easier to spot what cards people have as clients, or what's in the pool.  Some of the building artwork is attractive, or at least not bad.  But why oh why is the illustration for the Palace an MS Paint drawing of a man wearing nothing but a towel in front of a column?  Is this some sort of anti-Nero political cartoon?  Really, if they'd picked one art style, it would have been fine, but they've got five different ones, each with a different color palette, all crammed in the same little box.

RATING: 7.5
Glory to Rome, a game that became very, very popular in my Tally boardgame group.  I find this game to be an amped up on steroids version of San Juan in many ways.  The plusses are that there are numerous strategies and a myriad of powerful cards that can be played from the beginning of the game.  The downside is that Glory to Rome can be very chaotic at times with the gradual strategies and subleties found in San Juan and Race for the Galaxy tossed to the side(not as bad as Fluxx, but more than a few games have been roller coaster rides).  Overall, this game is a worthy and fun addition to a collection--now if they could only reprint it......

Suitability for Travel:

I have a 1 to 5 scale for four categories (1 is worst, 5 is best) in order of importance:

Portability (the less luggage space the better) - 4
Footprint (the less table space when playing the better) - 3
Scalability (the wider range of player counts the better) - 4
Learnability (the easier to teach non-gamers the better) - 2


Overall - 3.5

RATING: 8
This is a pretty unknown gem. Flawing arts in the beginning editions haven´t really helped it, but this is a 'gamers game' much meatier you can think at first look. There are many good things inside this 'goofy' game: many and often hard choices every turn, a fair amount of strategic paths (specially in 2P where you have more control), several endgame conditions. With 40 different buildings/powers, you must try different strategy/tactical choices every game, providing a great replayability. In spite of some lucky draws, you have plenty of opportunities for skillful play here, and you can definitly 'control your luck' with clever play. Every card in your hand may be played in 3 different ways (role, building, material)!, making many cutthroat decisions you have often to take. 

There are plenty of 'broken' powers in the buildings, but GtR is surprisingly well balanced. In some games, you will able to find solutions to counter your opponents combos, because buildings take usually several turns to build before being 'activated'. However other games will be a race, and the first player breaking the game will win. With 2P, I definitly recommend republican rules, 3 sites/color.

Original comment: I have no idea why this keeps getting compared to Race for the Galaxy. The two games are barely similar at all, only in the fact that both are card games that use the variable phase order mechanic, and have multiple uses for cards. I guess both games are supposed to occupy similar niches, but Race is deeper, more strategic, streamlined, and packs a ton of game into a short time fame, while Glory has more luck, has runaway leader problems, is overly fiddly, and overstays its welcome, having no clear end in sight and becoming tediously repetitive. I can see it improving after more plays, but for now I have to say that I am sincerely disappointed. I have only played once, so maybe I need to play more to appreciate it, but I would much rather play something else than spend my time playing this again.

RATING: 9
very very interesting and cool game. 
You try to gather the most victory points. I like the multirole cards. You can use them as an action, material, building ground or you can even "build" them and then use their specific action.
On your turn you will announce an action to take and everyone gets the decision to follow and make the same action or "think" and draw cards.
As soon as a certain amount of buildings are built or the building grounds run out, the game ends.
There are many roles for the gameplay but each goes very smoothly into gameplay, this game is perfectly balanced and playtested.
Huge recommendation on this one :)

RATING: 9
Glory to Rome, how I love thee!

One of my favorite games.

This is "Race for the Galaxy" on steroids: far more player interaction, more interesting (even insane) combos, and above all, way more laughter.

In its mechanics it's a Euro game through and through, but it produces the guffaws and the groans, the whooping and the curses, the delicious relish and the anxiety and all the drama normally associated with Ameritrash games. I.e., the best of both worlds. I cannot recommend it enough.

►ÉDITION : Filosofia, français, 2011.
Éditeur, nb joueurs : 2 à 5 joueurs.

RATING: 6
Let me start with the bad -- learning the game from the rulebook is a pain.  I had read through the rules before sitting down to play our first game, but what I really should have done was play a solo game before inflicting this on my opponent.  No joke, I heard YAWNING.  He swears it wasn't me, but hell, I was getting impatient with myself.  As a result, the learning curve on this game is a little steep.  If you're not playing this with some patient players, there's a good chance you'll ruin the game for them.  There's also the packaging, which I don't like at all.  There should be a more convenient way to package this game so that the cards aren't in three separate deck boxes in a plastic container.  I will be working on a method to condense this game ASAP....

Luckily, that's the worst I can say about the game.  The rest is an appealing little ditty along the lines of San Juan and Race for the Galaxy, two games I've taken a shine to over the past couple of years.  Glory to Rome takes that basic principle of the game and gives it more choices and more turn angst.

BASIC GAME: The basic game is challenging enough in its own right, and is a great way to learn how the game flows.  I think if we had tried playing with the special powers of the buildings for our first game, we would have been overwhelmed and may have given up on it.  But learning how to manage your hand, and develop a plan over a number of turns, is a nice challenge in and of itself.  I look forward to the advanced game, which I think will definitely improve upon a solid design.

ADVANCED GAME: The buildings' special powers change the game from being a tactical one to a strategic one, since the different buildings allow you to try for different win conditions and different card combos that will allow you to follow one path over another.  It adds an element of chaos to the proceedings, since the abilities are rule-breakers, but the added strategy makes that chaos worth it.  It's definitely more apparent to see the chances for strategy in this chaos than in Innovation's chaos.

1 play: 2 players
1 play: 3 players
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Let me start with the bad -- learning the game from the rulebook is a pain.  I had read through the rules before sitting down to play our first game, but what I really should have done was play a solo game before inflicting this on my opponent.  No joke, I heard YAWNING.  He swears it wasn't me, but hell, I was getting impatient with myself.  As a result, the learning curve on this game is a little steep.  If you're not playing this with some patient players, there's a good chance you'll ruin the game for them.

The Black Box edition is nice, but I didn't see it as being terribly necessary.  The artwork is nice, but it's easier to see the different types of cards using the more garish colors of the previous editions.  The improvement in card stock is nice, but I can see the edges of the cards scuffing up in a hurry.

Luckily, that's the worst I can say about the game.  The rest is an appealing little ditty along the lines of San Juan and Race for the Galaxy, two games I've taken a shine to over the past couple of years.  Glory to Rome takes that basic principle of the game and gives it more choices and more turn angst.

BASIC GAME: The basic game is challenging enough in its own right, and is a great way to learn how the game flows.  I think if we had tried playing with the special powers of the buildings for our first game, we would have been overwhelmed and may have given up on it.  But learning how to manage your hand, and develop a plan over a number of turns, is a nice challenge in and of itself.  I look forward to the advanced game, which I think will definitely improve upon a solid design.

ADVANCED GAME: The buildings' special powers change the game from being a tactical one to a strategic one, since the different buildings allow you to try for different win conditions and different card combos that will allow you to follow one path over another.  It adds an element of chaos to the proceedings, since the abilities are rule-breakers, but the added strategy makes that chaos worth it.  It's definitely more apparent to see the chances for strategy in this chaos than in Innovation's chaos.

1 play: 2 players (basic game)
1 play: 2 players (advanced game)

RATING: N/A
In 64 A.D., a great fire originating from the slums of Rome quickly spreads to destroy much of the city, including the imperial palace. Upon hearing news of the fire, Emperor Nero Caesar races back to Rome from his private estate in Antium and sets up shelters for the displaced population.
\13\\13\
Reporting directly to Nero, you are responsible for rebuilding the structures lost in the fire and restoring Glory to Rome.
\13\\13\
Glory to Rome is a card-based city building and resource management game with a novel mechanic. Each card may act as a building, a patron, a raw material, or a valuable resource, frequently forcing players into difficult decisions regarding how each card should be used. In addition, much of the game is played from the discard pool, giving players some control over what cards are accessible to opponents.
\13\\13\
Scoring is a combination of completing buildings and storing resources, with end-of-game bonuses for storing a diverse assortment. Game length is player-controlled, and is triggered in a few different ways.
\13\\13\
The lighthearted artwork and slim rulebook belie the strong strategic and tactical elements of Glory to Rome.

RATING: 9
Glory to Rome ticks every box on my list of things to love in a board/card game. Multi-use cards, engine building, asymmetric and rule-breaking card powers and multiple paths to victory. It also leans heavily on the design philosophy that as long as every card is over-powered, then none of them are. Much like Carl Chudyk's later design Innovation, this makes for some rather random and swingy gameplay in your early games. Once you've played through a few games, it becomes much easier to reign in the chaos and plot a course to victory, using the crazy card powers to accelerate your plans, or to slow down your opponents.

It's criminal that this has gone out of print. Hopefully that works itself out and get this back into player's hands as it is one of the few games worth of it's grail status.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Really neat card game. It's hard not to compare to the Puerto Rico card adaptations because of the role mechanic, but this one feels quite a bit different. I think GtR has a much better mitigation of randomness than San Juan or Race for the Galaxy in terms of what options you have available, and I really like the multiple game end conditions; they really add a lot of variability to the game. On the other hand, I've only played this with 2 players, and I suspect it would get really chaotic with more - maybe not. Still, a very fun game, and one I'd be happy to add to my collection despite already owning RFTG.

RATING: 7
Now have the "I.V." edition (2007).  Components are better, but still feel DTP. The box is nice. The artwork remains [b]terrible[/b]: a muted palette and public domain classical figures would be much better. You can feel the San Juan heritage, but it stands on its own (I like it better than SJ, certainly). Roles are constrained by your draw, and the economic cycle is clunky (but intriguing).  The multi-use of cards doesn't generate the angst of SJ or Race for the Galaxy, as the role lead/follow and the slower building pace makes your hand more transparent. But getting the sequence right and being adaptable is challenging and interesting.  The card combinations are great, but you can see untenable positions and runaway leaders: it's more swingy than either SJ or Race.

I had three concerns from the prior edition, and they're all still there: (a) a flat arc/the ease of forcing game end before functions become viable (esp. in 2-player); (b) no rules for trimming the deck size for < 5 players when a crucial part of the game is deciding when to end it; and (c) the Coliseum (it's overpowered, and the solution was not to balance it, but to increase the counter-cards, an approach I dislike as much here as I do in CCGs).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Card game where we citizens of Rome restore it to glory from a great disaster.  Try to control the communal pool of assets provided by the emperor. Gain influence and acquire clientele.  Cards are used for their resource, as clientele, or built as a structure with a power. Pretty heavy, dry and abstract for me.  Card art is very plain so there is no visual wow factor if that is important to you.

RATING: 10
Wow! While my box cover is slightly bubbly, this game game looks stunning! I'm thrilled with the card quality, the graphic design, the rules layout, and the great play aids. (Even the bonus game Plato3000 looks pretty, and like it might be alright.)

Glory to Rome is one hell of a brain exercise, you are faced with so many options, and incredibly tough choices. All while keeping a good pace, thanks in part to the Lead/Follow mechanism. The game rewards creative solutions, and inventive play, which are both qualities I adore in games. This is one of my all-time favorite games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Slightly disappointed after reading so many positive reviews for Glory to Rome claiming that it offered a lot more varied gameplay than Race for the Galaxy (especially since I'm currently kind of bored with the latter).  Although it's true that there are more options here, but it seems rather unbalanced with the forum being the most powerful and easiest way to victory.  It feels that you must play the game defensively if someone is clearly using that strategy.

Also, I found the symbols and instructions on the cards to be much less intuitive than the icons for Race.  I would even go as far to say the learning curve is much higher, and it'll definitely take more than two games before you can play it without the need to check the reference aide.  The gameplay is also quite restricting in comparison.  For example, an useless card in Race can simply be used as currency.  However, in Glory to Rome that is simply not the case.  You could find yourself holding something unwanted for a very long time with no way to exchange or even to discard it.

Lastly, I thought Glory to Rome was extremely fiddly.  Having to shuffle the cards around to indicate their various states was just such a chore to perform.  I'm not opposed to trying this out some more, but it certainly was not the replacement for Race as I had hoped.
